10.1016/j.jmp.2025.102907
No data was used for the research described in the article.

In recent years, a theoretical and practical effort has emerged to develop the procedural knowledge space theory (PKST;  Stefanutti, 2019 ) bridging the theories of problem spaces ( Newell & Simon, 1972 ) and knowledge spaces ( Doignon & Falmagne, 1985, 1999 ). PKST’s objective is to assess the problem-solving skills required in resolving a specific task. This is done using two deterministic models which are the problem space and knowledge space. The problem space represents the complete knowledge of the problem-solving task that is required for solving any problem in that task. On the other side, the knowledge space partially orders individuals based on their incomplete knowledge of the problem-solving task. Additionally, PKST aims to describe and, where possible, predict the observable behavior of a problem solver. This involves not only the description and prediction of accuracy (i.e., the correctness of the problem solution) but also the description and prediction of the single steps in the observable solution process undertaken by an individual when attempting to solve a given problem.

In the pursuit of these objectives, a probabilistic extension of the theories has been presented in the form of a model for the empirical validation of the knowledge spaces, called the  Markov solution process model  (MSPM;  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, & Brancaccio, 2021 ), and an adaptive assessment procedure ( Brancaccio, de Chiusole, & Stefanutti, 2023 ). In the five years since PKST was proposed some applications were developed. The most noteworthy one is PsycAssist, a web-based system for the assessment of fluid intelligence and executive functions, including problem-solving skills ( de Chiusole, Spinoso, Anselmi, Bacherini, Balboni, Mazzoni, Brancaccio, Epifania, Orsoni, Giovagnoli, Garofalo, Benassi, Robusto, Stefanutti, & Pierluigi, 2024 ).

The applications developed so far center around tasks that involve reaching a problem solution in the fewest possible steps for it to be deemed solved. Examples include the Tower of London test ( Shallice, 1982 ) and the Tower of Hanoi task, where problem solvers aim to transform an initial configuration into a goal configuration using the minimum number of moves. These tasks fall into the category of shortest-path problems, where the primary goal is to identify the shortest solution path. Within the framework of PKST, a specialized problem space known as the “shortest-path space” has been introduced ( Stefanutti et al., 2021 ) to effectively address this specific class of problems.

However, the shortest path problem is merely a subset of the broader category of optimization problems, which, in general, involves minimizing or maximizing the outcomes resulting from a choice or a sequence of choices. These problems are prevalent across various fields, including mathematics, computer science, engineering, economics, and cognitive science ( Boyd & Vandenberghe, 2004 ). In the cognitive science field, optimization problems have been employed to investigate problem-solving, decision-making, and the modeling of human behavior ( Davendra & Bialic-Davendra, 2020; MacGregor & Chu, 2011; van Rooij & Wareham, 2012 ). Additionally, numerous neuropsychological tests fall into the category of optimization problems. For example, the Porteus maze test ( Porteus, 1965 ) and the Halstead–Reitan Trail Making tests ( Battery, 1944 ) are instances where individuals are tasked with optimizing their strategies to achieve specific goals.

In this article, the concept of the shortest-path space is expanded to cover all well-structured optimization problems. In this extension called “optimizing path space”, particular attention is paid to considerations regarding local and global optima and their impact on the solution process. The goal is to provide a comprehensive framework within PKST that effectively models a broader spectrum of optimization problems. This framework lays the foundation for extending PKST to assess various cognitive and neuropsychological tests (e.g., maze-like tests, maps tests), experimental studies (e.g., traveling salesman problems), and puzzles of different complexities (e.g., Rubik’s Cube).

This paper is organized as follows: Section  2  provides the necessary mathematical background on KST and PKST. In Section  3  the shortest path space is generalized into an optimizing path space, accompanied by several theoretical results supporting this generalization. Section  4  shows the potential of our proposed approach by means of three examples: a maze-like problem and two delivery optimization problems. A summary and some final remarks are given in Section  5 .

The notion of problem space provided by  Newell and Simon (1972)  was used many times in the study of human problem-solving. A first link with Knowledge Space Theory was provided by  Stefanutti and Albert (2003) , and the mathematical foundations of Procedural Knowledge Space Theory (PKST) can be found in  Stefanutti (2019) . In this section, the main concepts of PKST are introduced.

Stefanutti et al. (2021)  introduced the notion of  goal space  to classify each step of the solution process of an individual as “correct-so-far” or “incorrect”. A goal space always contains a  goal state  
                         
                            g

                            f

                               s 
                               ∈ 
                               S 
                            
                          a problem solver encounters, the goal of the solution process is always the state  
                            g 
                         . When a problem solver arrives at the failure state  
                            f 
                         , the problem solution is considered wrong.

                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                         . It is worth mentioning that the only constraint for a move to be considered “correct” is that it belongs to a path that leads to the goal state (i.e., GS2). Moreover, depending on the type of application, there may be additional constraints to take into account.  Stefanutti et al. (2021)  considered a class of applications in which a problem is “solved” if and only if a solution is obtained with a minimum number of moves. The authors proposed a goal space containing only the solution paths at a minimum distance from the goal state, which is called the shortest path space.

To elaborate, the shortest path space refers to the correct way to solve a problem in all those applications in which it is required to complete the task in the fewest moves possible. Some examples are neuropsychological tests that investigate planning and problem-solving, like the Tower of London test ( Shallice, 1982 ), or mental games like the Tower of Hanoi that require the same abilities.

This section presents the main contribution of this article. It is about a special type of problem space, named the “optimizing path spaces”, which are goal spaces generalizing the notion of a shortest path space. In this last, every goal directed solution path has a “value”, which is measured by its length. The length, in turn, is an integer that counts the number of operations (or moves) in the path. Implicit in this definition is the idea that (i) every single move along a path has value 1, and (ii) the value of an entire path is the sum of the values of its moves. In this section, condition (i) is generalized by allowing a move to take on any real value.

The notion of a path value function is now introduced, which associates each path in  
                         T 
                       with a real weight.

Definition 4 
                         A  path value function  for  
                               T 
                             is any mapping  
                               
                                  v 
                                  : 
                                  T 
                                  → 
                                  R 
                               
                             such that: 
                         
                            
                               
                                  (PV1) 
                                  for every  
                                        
                                           a 
                                           ∈ 
                                           S 
                                        
                                     ,  
                                        
                                           v 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              a 
                                              , 
                                              ϵ 
                                              , 
                                              a 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           = 
                                           0 
                                        
                                     , 
                               
                               
                                  (PV2) 
                                  for every  
                                        
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              a 
                                              , 
                                              π 
                                              , 
                                              b 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           , 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              b 
                                              , 
                                              σ 
                                              , 
                                              c 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           ∈ 
                                           T 
                                        
                                     ,  
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 v 
                                                 
                                                    ( 
                                                    a 
                                                    , 
                                                    π 
                                                    σ 
                                                    , 
                                                    c 
                                                    ) 
                                                 
                                                 = 
                                                 v 
                                                 
                                                    ( 
                                                    a 
                                                    , 
                                                    π 
                                                    , 
                                                    b 
                                                    ) 
                                                 
                                                 + 
                                                 v 
                                                 
                                                    ( 
                                                    b 
                                                    , 
                                                    σ 
                                                    , 
                                                    c 
                                                    ) 
                                                 
                                                 , 
                                              
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (PV3) 
                                  for every nonempty subset  
                                        
                                           X 
                                           ⊆ 
                                           T 
                                        
                                      of solution paths,  
                                        
                                           v 
                                           
                                              [ 
                                              X 
                                              ] 
                                           
                                        
                                      has a minimum. 
                               
                            
                         
                         The path value function is  non-negative  if it maps  
                               T 
                             to the non-negative real numbers. Throughout the whole article, the convention is followed that the minimum of an empty set of real numbers is  
                               
                                  + 
                                  ∞ 
                               
                            .

The path value function is  non-negative  if it maps  
                               T 
                             to the non-negative real numbers. Throughout the whole article, the convention is followed that the minimum of an empty set of real numbers is  
                               
                                  + 
                                  ∞ 
                               
                            .

As a general remark, any non-negative path value function is monotone with respect to the sub-path relation. This immediately follows from the additivity Condition (PV2). Indeed, the monotonicity of the path value function is only guaranteed if this last is non-negative. In many interesting applications, including those exemplified in this article, the path value function is non-negative. However, there are cases in which the non-negative numbers turn out to be too restrictive, making the non-monotone path value functions more interesting and useful than the monotone ones. In this regard, the reader is referred to the example provided in Section  4.3 .

                             is named an  optimizing path space .

The mapping  
                         t 
                       established in  Definition 6  transforms a problem space into an optimizing path space. With this aim, it partitions the ternary relation  
                         T 
                       into three distinct classes: (i) the class of all the solution paths that are strict sub-paths of some optimal path; (ii) that of all those paths that are super-paths of some optimal path, and (iii) the class of all the remaining paths. For the paths in class (i), the mapping  
                         t 
                       is the identity, meaning that these particular paths always belong to the optimizing path space. Paths in class (ii) are “past the goal”, like in the path  
                         
                            
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     a 
                                     , 
                                     π 
                                     , 
                                     g 
                                     , 
                                     σ 
                                     , 
                                     b 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  . 
                               
                            
                         
                      These particular paths are all converted into optimal paths having the same initial state and same sequence of operations as the original path, whereas the final state is replaced by  
                         g 
                      . Finally, class (iii) collects all the remaining paths, each of which is mapped to a new path having its same initial state and sequence of operations, but ending up in the failure state  
                         f 
                      .

The next five lemmas and the subsequent theorem assure that the ternary relation  
                         
                            
                               T 
                            
                            
                               g

                         T 
                       via the mapping  
                         t 
                       of  Definition 6  is itself a problem space.  Lemma 2  assures that all paths not ending in the goal state are in

                               g

                                        T

Corollary 1 
                         
                            Any optimizing path space that satisfies condition (GS2) of  
                            
                               Definition  1  
                            is a goal space.

Any optimizing path space that satisfies condition (GS2) of  
                            
                               Definition  1  
                            is a goal space.

The proof for  Corollary 1  is omitted since it follows trivially from  Definition 1 . It is worth noticing that, in  Corollary 1 , condition (GS2) can always be obtained by reducing the problem space to a subset of it for which the condition itself is satisfied.

Corollary 2

In this section, three examples of applications for optimizing path spaces are provided. The first application is based on a maze-like test. This example aims to show how the optimizing path space can replace the shortest path space and be a more parsimonious model without losing information. The second is based on the class of delivery optimization problems. This example shows a possible application when the path value function, rather than being a distance, is the expected value of a random variable. In particular, the delivery problem is framed as a planning task in the context of a psychological experiment. The assignment is to minimize the average transition time between two cities on the map. The third example is also based on the class of delivery optimization problems. This example demonstrates an application with a non-monotone path value function, where both gains and losses are taken into consideration.

The shortest path space is suitable for applications that involve discrete steps, where each step is “meaningful” in the solution process. This means that the shortest path space is useful in applications where a clear and defined sequence of steps is necessary to solve the problem. For example, there is a whole class of problems (some of which, like the Tower of London and Tower of Hanoi are also psychological tests), where the length of a solution path coincides with the number of moves needed to reach the goal state from the initial state. That is, every single move weights one in the computations of the total length of the solution path. On the other side, there is a variety of other problems in which different moves have different weights in the computation of the total path length. An example of this is the class of maze-like tests, such as the Porteus Maze test ( Porteus, 1965 ), where a single move can be regarded as keeping walking through the maze until a crossroads is encountered. The length of this walk may change from one move to another depending on the shape of the maze. Therefore the computation of the total length of a solution path should take into account that each move weights differently.

Consider the maze displayed in  Fig. 3 . The starting point is on the bottom left side highlighted by the label “ s ”, whereas the exit of the maze is on the right side labeled “ g ”. The participant is required to move a square tile out of the maze. The side of the tile exactly measures the distance between the left and right walls of the corridors in the maze. An example of the tile is presented in green in the figure on top of the starting position  
                            s 
                         .

The directed graph, depicted in blue and overlying the maze, represents problem space  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     P

                                  S 
                                  , 
                                  Ω 
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        R 
                                     
                                     
                                        s 
                                     
                                  
                                  )

The nodes in the graph are black if multiple alternative routes depart from or arrive at them, and they are white otherwise. An optimal path in  
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  s

                            c

                            c 
                          from all the sub-paths. Thus, those problems are not equally difficult, but of an increasing order of difficulty. A second aspect, which is worth noticing in the optimizing path space, is that two or more solution paths with different lengths (i.e., number of operations) may have the same path value function.                          . To summarize, in applications like the one presented in this section a shortest path space, and the derived knowledge space, turn out to be unnecessarily complex. Instead, an optimizing path space seems to be more suitable for modeling these kinds of tasks. T The knowledge spaces derived from a shortest path space and an optimizing path space may be both validated on the same data set. This allows for empirical testing and comparisons of the two models and their underlying assumptions.

A second example of applications of the optimizing path space is the class of delivery problems. A traveling salesman problem (TSP;  Davendra & Bialic-Davendra, 2020 ) is a type of logistics problem that involves determining the most efficient routes for delivery vehicles to travel in order to deliver goods to a set of locations. The goal is to minimize the total distance or time needed to carry out all of the deliveries while also considering constraints such as vehicle capacity, delivery times, and road conditions.

Such problems are extremely well-known in fields like engineering, computer science, and mathematics ( Boyd & Vandenberghe, 2004 ). However, they can be used also as tests to assess planning and problem-solving ability (e.g.,  MacGregor & Chu, 2011 ). For instance, it is possible to set up an experiment in which a participant is presented with the problem in  Fig. 5 .

This problem has a problem space

                               =

                            Ω

Another important aspect of this example is that the path value function is the expected value of a random variable, specifically the time taken to go from one city to another. The use of an expected value as a path value function raises several theoretical and empirical questions. On the theoretical side, one question is how to formally define the relationship between the two. On the empirical side, it opens up the possibility of applying the optimizing path spaces to situations where the variable to be optimized is a random variable. Finally, TSPs, such as the one presented in this section, can be interesting applications for testing planning abilities in real-life scenarios while maintaining a controlled and manipulable environment. An example is provided in  Fig. 5 , where all the cities are depicted at the same distance, although the average times required to move from one city to another are not equal. This scenario can be compared with one where distances and times are directly proportional. It is reasonable to assume that finding the optimal solution path becomes less challenging when the difference in average times is represented both graphically and numerically, rather than only numerically.

In all the examples provided in the previous sections, the path value function is non-negative. In this section an example is illustrated where the assumption of non-negativity does not hold. Non-negative path value functions cannot be applied in situations where the final payoff is the result of costs and benefits, which are inherent to many economic applications. In this section, the running example presented in Section  3  is contextualized into a delivery problem, with costs and benefits.

Considering the problem state  
                            d 
                          as the goal state, application of the mapping  
                            t 
                          in  Definition 6  results in the optimizing path space (see  Fig. 2 ) that minimizes the cost of returning to the depot. This scenario is more complicated than the previous ones, as the optimal paths are not determined by minimizing a single attribute but by balancing a combination of losses and gains. Specifically, taking detours from the shortest routes to complete additional deliveries, increasing overall fuel and vehicle usage costs, but this can be well compensated for by the additional money gained from these deliveries.

In conclusion, this research results in further advancements in procedural knowledge space theory by extending the notion of shortest path space to a wide range of optimization problems beyond minimum distance. The foundation of such extension is due to the implementation of any arbitrary evaluation function, as long as the value of a solution path can be reduced to the sum of the values of every single move that constitutes it. Thus, the optimizing path space widened the applicability of PKST models. To demonstrate the versatility of the approach, three concrete examples of application have been presented in this article. In all cases, it is shown that the distance between two nodes in a graph does not provide a good indication of the optimal solution for the task.

In the first example, the distance covered in the maze is discretized as a shortest path space, in a way in which each operation coincides with a single step, and as an optimizing path space in a way in which each operation is weighted with the taxicab distance required to go from one landmark to another. In this section, we argue that the result obtained with the shortest path space is redundant and ineffective, compared to the optimizing path space and it can lead to misleading interpretation. In the second example, a simple traveling salesman problem is presented in which it is required to optimize the delivery routes between two cities. We show how the optimizing path space can be used to model this class of problems. The traveling salesman problems are a class of optimization problems that receive attention both in computer science and cognitive psychology. Several experimental studies have used the TSP to study the human abilities to generate a global optimum in the function of the available information ( Kong & Schunn, 2007 ). The third example introduces a real-world application of a non-monotone path value function. In complex scenarios, the path value function must account for both gains and losses, which is particularly relevant in economic applications, as demonstrated in this last example.

The approach presented in this article can be directly applied in this line of studies by developing a paradigm in which the path value function underlying the derivation of the optimizing path space is manipulated according to the hypothesis. For instance, two evaluation functions could be thought of, one which favors global optimization, and the other which favors local optimization. This will directly result in two different optimizing path spaces for the same task and as a consequence in two different knowledge spaces. The fit of these knowledge spaces to data can be tested by means of existing probabilistic models such as the basic local independence model (BLIM;  Falmagne & Doignon, 1988 ) and the MSPM ( Stefanutti et al., 2021 ). Moreover, their relative fit can be compared using standard model comparison indexes, such as the Akaike Information Criterion or the Bayesian Information Criterion.

The approach presented in this article focuses on optimization as a minimization problem. It is important to note that the same results can be easily applied to maximization problems by considering the dual. For instance, the first step would involve defining a path value function to have a maximum.

This work is built upon the theoretical framework of knowledge assessment provided by PKST. However, optimization extends beyond this realm, intersecting with various other disciplines. Future investigations could explore the theoretical connections between the path value function delineated in this study and concepts such as the Bellman equations in dynamic programming (see e.g.,  Bertsekas, 2015 ) or the mini-max algorithm in combinatorial game theory (see e.g.,  Beck, 2008 ).

Furthermore, it is essential to recognize that the theoretical framework presented here is focused only on “additive” path value functions. Therefore, there is the potential for a further generalization of the theory to accommodate other types of evaluation functions, taking into account that with “non-additive” path value functions, (some of) the theoretical results presented in this study could not hold anymore.
